Honestly 30 lumens is not bad at all for the average task people use a Mini Mag for it works great. After you install the LED chances are you will never have to replace it again. B00979CS50|4.0|As one of the few performers - perhaps the only one - to be sued by his label for producing \\"unrepresentative music,\\" it should be clear to all and sundry that Neil Young will issue what he damn well pleases. Perhaps less apparent to more casual visitors to his extensive catalog is the cyclical nature of his output, which is both a comment on and a tribute to the enormous breadth of the man's talent which allows him to operate in multiple music genres with equivalent success. This pattern has its positive and negative effect for those not interested or willing to follow Mr. Young on his travels down the musical highway and - to paraphrase the man himself - into the ditch at the side of the road. Those loving his country side can be put off by his garage band material, while those wishing it was only Neil and the Horse might feel the same about the country product. Then there's the far more eclectic material - his forays into `alternative' styles (`Trans' and `Le Noise' being two examples separated by over 20 years) - that can alienate a wide audience, including David Geffen. Suffice it to say, the small group loving Neil Young the composer of the broadest scope of music imaginable is the only segment always satisfied.<br /><br />I'd suggest the last decade tested even this group; his last four or five albums have been somewhat mediocre and, on a couple of occasions, highly politicized. Throughout this period, his label stuck with him, though it's probably the case they had little or no choice other than dropping him. So, one can only imagine what - despite the history - Reprise executives thought when they first heard of `Americana.' But, the bigger surprise may have been when they heard it. While still a point off the notional curve, the album was a brilliant rendering of American music as reinvented by Neil Young with Crazy Horse. Now, it's been a long time since he recorded with these guys and some believed - myself included - that they'd never play together again. But, they did get together and record, and now we have a second album; one that is a triumph.<br /><br />`Psychedelic Pill' is a two-CD, 88 odd minute return to the glory that can be Neil Young and Crazy Horse. It evokes the same sigh of pleasure and relief one felt when first hearing `Ragged Glory,' itself a return from another stylistic adventure. Given Young's tendency to write songs and then stow them away for years and decades, it's hard to know or imagine or guess when any of the material may have been written. For example, the first cut (`Driftin' Back') feels a lot like it was done during the `Sleeps With Angels' / Complex Sessions period. After starting over an acoustic guitar and vocal solo, the band joins in for a 27 odd minute journey that made me really want to be driving north on Highway One past Pismo Beach and Avila Beach heading for Santa Cruz. This trip gives you time and situation to think about things and delve even the whys and hows of it all. This is why the thrust of the song seems to be recovery and reunion on multiple levels. Clearly Neil Young wants to reconnect with his garage side and his long-time (40 plus years) partners; that can be seen by the sharp shift from the aforementioned solo to what is an archetypical NY & CH production. It also appears to be a yearning for past things lost - another concept which permeates much of his music. But, this emotion is heightened by lyrics that - while a bit opaque and inserted periodically throughput the 27 odd minutes of the piece - reference his recently released autobiography; his long-standing and well-known hated of digital (note the CDs were recorded in A - A - D format; who does that anymore???) and the potential decline and fall of civilization due to corporate collectivism, among other things.  Yes, overall, he seems a bit peeved with the current state of things; again, not an unusual commentary from him. Let's say, it's epic in its scope and a fine entre into this recording as it tells us Young wants to get some things out in the open.<br /><br />The two versions of `Psychedelic Pill,' one in the second slot and the other at cut nine is a defiant scream that being old (I'm 63; he's 67) doesn't mean acting that way. Built around another of those Neil Young mythic women the song orders us to follow her lead and live life to the fullest.<br /><br />On `Ramada Inn,' we have Neil Young and Crazy Horse evoking the sound of 'Touch the Night,' from the Geffen period `Landing on Water\\" CD. A couple is passing the night just outside of Los Angeles. If understood correctly, they're moving south and are firmly bonded by their purpose of visiting long-time friends. Dinner and drinks and still more drinks gradually exposes the horror of the relationship. At the least, the man is an alcoholic; at worse he's an abuser. The woman - through inaction arising from fear, or desperation or something - stays in the relationship. There are several times in the song where we are informed the man \\"gets what he wants\\" and the woman \\"does what she needs;\\" with the unspoken message being \\"to survive.\\"<br /><br />`Born in Ontario' returns to a retrospective theme with a tune that is the least psychedelic of the set. While he's talked of growing up in Canada before on `Helpless,' this piece seems again to have been driven by thought processes related to his autobiography. He references both his parents and their travels - any maybe travails - as he was growing up. Pushed along by a countryish melody with a chorus built around listening to the Grateful dead, it is, to my mind, it's the least substantial song here.<br /><br />The substantiality is greater on the second piece describing how Young got into song writing. With `Twisted Road,' we learn of the impact of two giants (yes, it's a pun but you have to wait for it) on Neil's song-writing. The song begins by mentioning the how Dylan's `Like A Rolling Stone' caught his attention and the connective leap he made to Hank Williams. Having not yet read his autobiography, I don't know if this topic is mentioned as a notional moment-of-creation. I will say that upon hearing the words, it made perfect sense to me.<br /><br />And now, to loop in the pun with my view of \\"Walk Like A Giant,' another song that is part of the look back that permeates the entire two CDs of material. It's what this song is not that makes it so powerful. It's not a rant and it's certainly not a whine. Nor is it a belly-aching bitch. In point of fact, I'm not sure I can describe what it is with any certainty until several - perhaps many more listens - so that I can divorce my feelings of what getting old means from the message Young is making. What you will find is an instrumental digression at the end that - along with three false endings and what sounds like the base line to `Like A Hurricane,' approach `ARC' in its organized chaos.<br /><br />`She's Always Dancing' is one of those enigmatic Young songs, depicting admiration from afar. Likely more metaphor than reportage, it's another recurring theme harking to other remarkable and perhaps unobtainable women as portrayed in `Cinnamon Girl.'<br /><br />Shock, pain and the inability to solve the problems affecting your family makes `For the Love of Man' the most sad and poignant song that Young has perhaps ever written. It addresses the agony a parent, any parent would feel when confronted with insurmountable barriers like those his son has faced throughout his life. The song reaches its pinnacle in the last line when Young - a man always seemingly in control, or at least determined enough to push on despite the damage he may cause - simply admits that parents often find themselves unable to help their children they way the really want to do. But, they often find the strength to do what they need to.<br /><br />All in all, this record is classic Neil Young and Crazy Horse. Though the lyrics may be a little suspect, one cannot deny the magic that happens in the synergyof these four guys.ms. Having not yet read his autobiography, I don't know if this topic is mentioned as a notional moment-of-creation. I will say that upon hearing the words, it made perfect sense to me.<br /><br />And now, to loop in the pun with my view of \\"Walk Like A Giant,' another song that is part of the look back that permeates the entire two CDs of material. B005HSGB6W|5.0|I had heard of this movie from a friend many years ago. From what I'd heard of it, it was a graphic story laden with violence. I also learned that it was based on a book. Years later, still have never seeing it, I saw that my local library at the time carried the movie. So I checked it out and prepared myself for a controversial film. (I was in the midst of watching a lot of other classics at that time that I had never seen: One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest, Pscyho, Gone with the Wind, etc.) This was around 2009 or 2010.<br /><br />What really ended up surprising me was that I really liked the idea behind the movie. I didn't care for the violence or any of that, obviously, but the central idea behind it all really won me over and really stuck with me well after I had turned it off and moved on. And what was this idea? It was the classic issue of morality. Free will. Good and bad. Moral choice.<br /><br />The gist of the story is, if a society had the ability to \\"rehabilitate\\" sadistic and violent individuals, at what cost would that be justified? Because, ultimately, after Alex receives his treatment, he may no longer have been able to inflict harm on others, but he was also rendered incapable of choice. In addition, he was incapable defending himself against the evils of the world, since he was conditioned to be intolerant of any kind of violence.<br /><br />So this leads one to wonder, if this type of treatment existed, would it be a fair course of action? Might some justify it as payback for truly sadistic persons who have inflicted so much harm upon others? If given the choice of this \\"treatment\\" versus a life incarcerated, or even the death penalty, what would someone in Alex's shoes choose if they were truly informed of the implications of such conditioning? We certainly become aware that Alex comes to regret his choice and loathes the \\"rehabilitation\\" he recieved, but he was never truly informed of the specifics of his treatment. Ultimately, he finds his quality of life so intolerable after the treatment that he sees the only possible way to resolve his situation is to off himself.<br /><br />In 2011 I got my hands on a used copy of this book. Ever since viewing the movie, which, as I mentioned earlier, stuck with me, I was eager to read the literary work that was responsible for inspiring the film. The edition I picked up was the one most recently published in the US, the one that included the original 21st chapter that was published in Britain but excluded from previous copies here in the states. Therefor, Kubrick's film, though filmed in England, sticks to the American version - the version that ended with the 20th chapter - instead of the 21st. (More on the 21st chapter later.)<br /><br />The first thing that caught me off guard when I first began reading the book, that I had no knowledge or expectation of, was the language. The language! Surely I've read books with some unusual use of language, old english, slang, etc., but the language in this book was a different breed altogether. The language Alex speaks in, while essentially English, is so obscure and filled with such odd words that it hardly resembles English at times. It's called Nadsat, and it is the teen slang of the future. This in itself was intimidating. Just a few pages into the book, I was quite nervous about what I'd gotten myself into.<br /><br />Sure enough, I got the hang of it eventually. A lot of it can be figured out in context. A few chapters into the book I ended up looking for a glossary type resource online, and found one. This helped a lot with determining the meaning of some of the tougher words. The truth is, though, if you go into the book and treat it like submersion in to a foreign language, you'll understand enough, do just fine, and ultimately figure it out. Of course, if you want to pause and look up every word, too, this will eventually work out fine as well, since by the time you're half way through the book you have an excellent grip on Nadsat. (P.S. Any one want to govoreet in Nadsat with me? I'm real horrorshow at it now.)<br /><br />It's been a few years now since I've seen the movie. From what I can remember, the movie seems to be quite true to the book. Though I am curious, had Kubrick incorporated the 21st chapter into his film, how that would have been done. I would love to see yet another director take on this work of fiction and put it on the big screen yet again in a fresh new interpretation.<br /><br />So, that 21st chapter. I said I'd get back to it, didn't I? Well, though I appreciate that Alex eventually comes around and wants to change (a nice fairytale ending), I have a few problems with it. The thing is this: yes, kids can be irresponsible, reckless, explosive, impulsive, and violent. But I don't believe that all kids are this way, and the ones that are all exhibit these traits to varying degrees, and most not to the extent that Alex was. The level of violence exhibited by Alex was in no way a mandatory or common component of youth. Alex was above and beyond the troubled teenager. And, in my opinion, Alex demonstrated psychopathic characteristics. (I'm no phsycologist - this is all just my opinion.) He got his kicks from other people's suffering and demonstrated little to no remorse for his actions. In addition, he was incredibly egocentric and narcissistic. So, for Burgess to suddenly have Alex overcome this youthful lust for destruction, and crave the more productive route of creation, seems a little too \\"out of the blue,\\" if you know what I mean. Especiallly given his propensity towards violence throughout the entire book. It bothers my that Burgess implies that youth=an inclination towards detruction, and maturity=an inclination towards creation. It just doesn't work that way, to me. Maybe in this strange, distopian, nadsat-speaking world Burgess fashioned, but not in this world. In spite of my criticism, I realize why Burgess wrote the 21st chapter this way, as he said himself, \\"the twenty-first chapter gives the novel the quality of genuine fiction, an art founded on the principle that human beings change.\\" The problem is, I would have liked the change to have been more authentic and convincing.<br /><br />Regardless of the 21st chapter, this is a fascinating story of a thuggish protagonist with some serious issues, and the curious path he follows which brings into question the matter of moral choice, free will, and a human's inclination towards good and evil. A classic tale, for certain, with some crazy language mixed in. Now I have to go find the movie again and see what I think of it now.rip on Nadsat. (P.S. Any one want to govoreet in Nadsat with me? B008JMKN4Y|4.0|I picked up Beautiful Disaster knowing that there was some controversy surrounding the story. While it's labeled as a YA book, many readers and reviewers alike are upset because of the racy content; Sex, violence, gambling and unhealthy relationships fill this book and possibly the minds of the young readers who are drawn to this book. Is it mislabeled or misjudged?<br /><br />Beautiful Disaster tells the story of Abby Abernathy and bad boy Travis Maddox. After leaving her hometown in Kansas, Abby and her best friend go away to school at Eastern University to start new lives where no one knows of her family and her past. When she thinks that she's finally got a new life established, she quite literally runs into Travis Maddox, Eastern University's bad boy. Their relationship is passionate and tumultuous, but is he worth her image and the careful life she's built for herself?<br /><br />This was not my favorite book of all time, but I did really enjoy it. I read it all in one day and I have to say that I was really happy after I finished reading the story. I'd seen the book everywhere and while it took me a while to finally pick it up, I'm glad I waited until I was in the mood to start. This was one of those books where if i had started reading it and wasn't ready, I would never have sat down to finish it.<br /><br />I really liked the characters in this story. While Abby was a little bit messed up, she's still a strong woman with morals and a strong sense of who she wants to be (not  necessarily who she is at the moment or how to get to where she wants to be... she has some issues with that). Travis was the typical douche-bag, who also happens to be a big man-slut. He slept with anyone and everyone and made no apologies for it. Together, they made a very interesting dynamic. You saw Abby and Travis grow together and change because of each other. Abby became more adventurous and less cloistered. She wasn't as scared of the world when she was with Travis and he on the other hand changed completely. He became a one woman man, was sweet, he never pressured Abby and he protected her.<br /><br />Of course we saw them fall apart, but as you can imagine, there was a happy ending. This was a sort of feel good book that ended with you smiling and feeling all gushing inside. I love when books make me feel that way.<br /><br />The plot of the story was not as original as I would have liked, but I wasn't bored. It was a lot to happen in one story and the end was a little bit rushed, but other than that, the pacing was pretty spot on.<br /><br />Now I do understand why there is some controversy over this being a YA book. There is violence and sex in this book and it wouldn't be appropriate for young readers... BUT (and it's a pretty big but) this book was aimed at an older YA audience. It's about a college aged girl who finds love and explores it. College aged young adult readers, in my opinion, are fully equipped to handle the more mature themes in this book. I mean, when I think of what I was doing in college, this could have been a story about me. I went to parties, I had a steady boyfriend and I didn't always make the best choices.<br /><br /> In regards to young readers picking this book up and reading something inappropriate, all they need to do is read the back of the book and look where they found it. I did not find this book in my local book store in the YA section, I found it in the romance section. I also (as I always have done since I was a kid) read the back of the book and it told me that there was sex and violence in this book and I made the decision that Beautiful Disaster was appropriate for me.<br /><br />All babbling aside, I truly enjoyed this book. It was interesting and heart warming at parts and I would recommend it to those who like a good romance rife with teen angst, frat parties and motorcycles. It's a fun ride!<br /><br />8 out of 10<br /><br />Happy Reading :)other hand changed completely. B009FBX5M0|4.0|I was initially skeptical when I saw that Newman was scoring the new Bond movie.  He is extremely talented, but has a distinct style and I've never heard him do an action soundtrack.  When I began listening to the first track, \\"Grand Bazaar, Istanbul,\\" I thought for five minutes that this score would be spectacular.  The action cues are sensational and the Monty Norman bond motif makes subtle appearences making me think that we would get much much more.  Throughout the entire soundtrack I was teased with the possibility of a crescendo of the classic bond theme.  My favorite Bond soundtracks were always the David Arnold ones, especially Tomorrow Never Dies and World is Not Enough.  He masterfully combined action music with the classic bond theme.  I always knew that was Bond on the screen when that music was playing.  Obviously as they shifted in a new direction with the series so too did the music.  Casino Royale and Quantum of Solace hinted at the return of the Bond Theme, but never delivered.  This set up Newman to create a truly epic score.  Unfortunately he comes up short.  Despite his best efforts and working out of his element, Newman succeeds only in copying David Arnold's Casino Royale Score.<br /><br />Mixed with the action are Newman's signature strings and beautiful orchastral talents.  This felt out of place in a bond movie, but works well on a stand alone soundtrack.  Overall, this is the best bond score this century (which isn't saying much).  The most infuriating track is \\"Breadcrumbs\\" in which it plays the beginning of the Norman Bond theme and builds and builds...to nothing.  It's like the track was cut off for no reason symbolizing both the potential and the reality of the soundtrack.  I will continue to be unsatisfied until I get the Monty Norman Bond theme mixed into the score like the classic David Arnold days.

B001BSBBEE|5.0|Carnival of Souls(1962) is a solid, surreal, low budget horror movie filmed in black and white. It was not a big hit in 1962, but late night TV airings helped the film reach cult status. Everything from The Sixth Sense(1999) to The Frighteners(1996) borrowed ideas from this movie. Carnival of Souls was directed by Herk Harvey. He made dozens of educational shorts for Centron. In Carnival of Souls, Mary Henry(Candace Hilligoss) survives a nasty car accident caused by some reckless drag racers! She moves to Utah and gets a job as a church organ player. Mary can see pale ghosts walking around. There are moments where people ignore her. Director Herk Harvey plays a ghost known as \\"The Man\\". Sidney Berger plays John Linden, an oily, charismatic guy who is attracted to Mary. Carnival of Souls is a creepy, atmospheric horror film that's low on violence. It feels \\"dream like\\" at times. It has an organ driven music score. Carnival of Souls was made with good taste and it's tame enough to show on TV. Filmmakers such as David Lynch and George Romero are fans of this film. A \\"remake\\" of Carnival of Souls was made in 1998 and it went straight to home video. The remake starred Bobby Phillips from The X-Files and Stargate SG-1. Some people have pointed out that Carnival of Souls feels like a Twilight Zone episode. I guess that's true. Candace Hilligoss is decent as Mary Henry. She was a really good looking B-movie starlet. Hilligoss looks lovely as she sees \\"The Man\\" staring at her from the first floor of a staircase. I first saw Carnival of Souls on a Netflix rental in 2005. 0393339882|5.0|\\"Does the moon exist only when you look at it ?\\" asked Einstein.  This forms the central philosophical battle that Manjit Kumar's book traces through the history of the quantum from 1900 until today.  On one side is Einstein, the cultural icon who also happens to be perhaps the greatest Physicist of all time and on the other side is Bohr, a physics icon who among the inner circle may be of equal stature.  Einstein could not believe in a world without the realism that seems to be true in the macro world but Bohr felt nature is as the quantum describes it - probabilistic, non deterministic and most importantly dependent on an observer.  Joining them in battle are the great physicists of the 20th century whose names live immortalized as constants, measures, principles and equations.  Kumar mixes in equal parts biography making this an interesting read beyond the science.  Although much has been learned since 1900 and so much of our man-made material lives are a result of the understanding these individuals gained from the quantum world we still cannot really answer Einstein's question.  We are all made of quantum stuff and believe in our reality and determinism but all that we are made of does not seem to follow these rules.  A wonderful journey through a mind boggling world.

B000S0GYRU|4.0|This movie is a bit of a puzzler.<br /><br />The story has a lot of promise, but I feel that it wasn't exploited in the best way in this script. It seemed to lack focus, containing a number of superfluous characters that could have been excised to make way for more footage with our heroine and the key antagonists.<br /><br />The acting suffers from the same malaise that all Italian movies of the time did - the shooting in multiple languages and dubbing-in of English (in this case) later gives the delivery a certain remote quality. You get the same almost caricature delivery in spaghetti westerns.<br /><br />There are also some idiosyncratic things that arrived sideways for me until explained in the bonus disc. I generally disapprove of nuance that requires an explanation to avoid derailing the movie. Movies should stand on their own. If you have to explain something for it to make sense you filmed it wrong in the first place (unless surrealism was the intent of course).<br /><br />The movie is also hampered in that the state of the art for SFX in Italy at the time was not that of Hollywood, which was falling off the brink of Computer Assisted camerawork and the infant CGI techniques that would soon replace older, less satisfying techniques.<br /><br />That said, this is my favorite Argento movie. The idea is a good one, the set-piece scenes are well composed if sometimes overdone (I sometimes think this film might have been much stronger if filmed a-la Hitchcock rather than a-la Chainsaw) though the sheer exuberance of the blood-and-guts has its own charm.<br /><br />The young actresses are gorgeous, especially the female lead, who has eyes you can just fall into.<br /><br />I believe this was also the last occidental movie to be made in technicolor. The use of color in this film is striking, but I looked in vain for any sort of message in the choice of colors. Very clever, though, and I think the explanation of how it was done was probably the best part of the bonus disc for me.<br /><br />Four stars for effort.s disc for me.<br /><br />Four stars for effort.

He didn't intend this of course. It's interesting to see that over the 12 editions of Origin of Species, Darwin moved from his initial belief in natural selection to a Lamarckian version. Had Darwin bothered to read Gregor Mendal's paper on hybrid cultivation by cross breeding -- which led to the discovery of genes -- he might have modified his theories a bit. Mendal's report was found among Darwin's papers, pages uncut and unread.<br /><br />The decline of Western Civilization was not a direct result of Darwin's writings; but evolved from the ideas of those who tried to apply  evolution science to \\"social science\\" in order to control people's lives. Blade Runner for example, deviates quite significantly from Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep and yet remains one of the greatest SF films of all time because it remains true to the novel's bleak view of our planet and its thematic question -- Who is more human, the humans or the androids? The elimination of Deckard's wife, the almost complete disappearance of Mercerism change the story significantly, but do not alter it conceptually.<br /><br />This movie fails miserably in portraying the true pathos of The Hunger Games. So many key thematic elements are dropped or left unexplained to the non-reader that there is no way you can get to the story's true meaning without having read the series. And if you have read the series, then you will be mystified by the way they gloss over the true meaning of the Mockingjay, the impetus behind the scenes in the cave, and much more. Replacing District 11's tribute to Katniss (the care package) with a simple salute and a riot (which belongs in the second book) takes away one of the most important thematic events in the tale and twists it into something completely different.<br /><br />The key here is supposed to be how Katniss's simple acts of heroism and respect for her fellow human beings bring about a complete change in the people viewing them -- how they change from fans of a sporting event to admirers of her nobility. How they begin to view their own actions in light of hers. It never happens. B009JNXK88|5.0|First of all, let me say that the gaming world would consider me a \\"core gamer\\"; I've been playing video games for over 25 years, and have had basically every home console released state-side since the mid-80's. I play between 10-20 hours of video games a week, if not more when I'm really into something. When touch-screen games started to rear their ugly head I was disgusted, as no amount of smashing my thumb against a glass surface would ever give me the tactile satisfaction and fine-level of control that an array of buttons and a joystick could. Several of my favorite games have even been adapted to the touch-screen medium, most notably Mega Man X, but with such terrible results -- the touch-screen controls do the original game a great disservice -- that I'm going to try and forget that they ever existed.<br /><br />For the most part, I really dislike touch-screen games; I find them to be an unfortunate step backwards in the often ultra-responsive controls which gaming has evolved over the last few decades, and as a \\"core\\" gamer, control response is crucial to any gaming experience.<br /><br />This brings me to League of Evil, which I downloaded because I'm always trying to expand my horizons by giving things (touch-screen games) another chance, because I love action/platform games, and  ... because it was free, of course! Anyway, I am really impressed with League of Evil. This game just might have the tightest, most responsive controls of any touch-screen action game currently on the market, and to beat the time goals on these levels you really need responsive controls. Someone mentioned that this is like Super Meat Boy, and I definitely get that vibe from this game; it's a challenging action-platform game which demands quick reflexes and thinking.<br /><br />The only problem I have with this game ... is that I can't play it with buttons. I hate mashing my thumbs against an unforgiving glass surface, but that's not really a complaint about the game as much as it is a complaint about the medium by which the game is delivered, so I have not subtracted any stars for that. However, I will subtract a star for the fact that the arrow \\"buttons\\" are too far apart; the game sometimes demands quick direction changes; it's really easy to lose track of towards which button you are closest, to not shift far enough in the desired direction, which means you end up going (or continuing in) the opposite direction you intended. Why don't game designers make touch-screen \\"buttons\\" positionable and sizable by the user? At least there is no dead-zone between the buttons, so with practice it's easy to find the sweet-spot between the buttons to really make some fast directional changes. I wish I could just subtract half a star and make this 4.5 stars, because these kinds of control nuances become more familiar with more game play, and the more I play this the less it bothers me.<br /><br />(Actually, forget it; I am going to round-up and just give this 5 stars, because it really is a great game, especially considering the price.)<br /><br />Fortunately, there's ample opportunity to familiarize yourself with League of Evil's controls, as it has over 160 levels. I'm really glad I got this for free during the \\"Free App of the Day\\" promotion Amazon does, but I would gladly pay the usual price for this if necessary. Overall this is a solid action-platform game, and the quick levels (usually less than 30 seconds each with a skilled play-through) are perfect for honing your fine motor skills on the go. There's ample replay value, as each level has a star rating (out of three) based upon the player's finish time, as well as a special out-of-the-way pickup for each level.<br /><br />I highly recommend League of Evil for any fan of retro action-platform goodness.medium by which the game is delivered, so I have not subtracted any stars for that. Somehow, the song doesn't feel repetitive.  Perhaps the live setting makes it feel that way.  \\"Little Richard Medley\\" is about what you'd expect- an energetic live performance of several Little Richard songs grouped together (a famous tradition for live rock artists back in the day).  It is what it is, basically.  The vocal melodies aren't really better than the originals, nor worse.  The short bursts of guitar and sax work stands out the most for its melodic nature.<br /><br />\\"Poor Elijah- Tribute to Johnson\\" really showcases Bonnie Bramlett's vocal range, and the Jerry Garcia-like vocals of Delaney are a nice addition.  What *really* makes this song so great is the guitar solo in the middle.  Talk about a splendid one!  Unfortunately the song does lose some of its magic when Bonnie stops taking advantage of her range and dwindles down to a repetitive chorus with the line \\"Nobody's here\\".  \\"I Don't Want To Discuss It\\" has a fast-moving pace and vocals with a scorching guitar solo in the middle.  Truth be told, I don't really think these vocals are *that* great and I can certainly do without the repeating line \\"You're My Girl\\" that borders on clumsily sung.  \\"That's What My Man Is For\\" has exceptionally vibrant vocals courtesy of Bonnie.  If this is her interpretation of gospel blues similar to Carole King, it's wonderful.<br /><br />Overall, pretty impressive but nothing that leaves me wanting more.  This is all the Delaney & Bonnie I'm looking for.

Here, chilled out Kylie stands totally centre stage, the clever musical backing (together with fine backing vocalists) supporting her rather than carrying her.  Her voice, a curious mix of youthfulness and adult understanding infuses the lyrics with fresh meaning and draws out their reflective qualities. Never Too Late is Kylie's torch song and she channels a vampish Marilyn Monroe in Slow.  I Believe in You is an affirmative lullaby and dance smash On a Night Like This morphs into a bluesy swagger.....this is a listening journey worth taking.

This movie is a mess. I get that it was trying to capture the feel of a 60's romantic thriller, like Charade, but it fails miserably. As my review title says, go watch Charade instead. Audrey Hepburn and Cary Grant were two of the most glamourous stars of their time. Hepburn was beautiful and when it came to fashion was one of the most stylish women around. Cary Grant was suave and super cool, it's easy to see anyone falling for his charms. I'd love it if they'd just release this movie theaters. They used to do that more often, but now you can only see an older movie in theaters if you're lucky enough to have a theater that plays them. I don't think I really need to go into the plot of this movie, there are plenty out there and of course there was the trailer for it as well. I recall this movie got a lot of promotions and even had giant cardboard displays at theaters. And why not, it stars two of the biggest actors today, Johnny Depp and Angelina Jolie.<br /><br />This part is gonna stir up some controversy because of what I'm about to say; I'm not a fan of Angelina Jolie. I've never seen the hype of her as an actress. And this has nothing to do with the whole Jennifer Aniston/Brad Pitt thing; Hollywood has plenty of this kind of drama going on that it's nothing new. I admit I enjoyed her in Girl, Interrupted, she did a great job in that movie as well as the rest of the cast. In fact her earlier work is way better given that she did riskier roles compared to now. Just about every role she takes on is just very standard Hollywood, and when she was first getting big she was doing all kinds of unconventional roles. I recommend seeing the movie Gia where she plays a drug addicted model, it's a sad movie (based on a true story as well) but it's well made and acted. I also enjoy her in the Kung Fu Panda movies, although the major highlights of those movies come from the action and comedy. But in a way those movies go with her conventional roles of playing a tough as nails women like in Lara Croft or Wanted. So yes, she's done some good movies here and there. But for the most part, she's just meh. She rarely does a lot of emoting in movies, and giving a cold stare the whole time doesn't mean you're acting and giving any real depth. And sure I can see that she's an attractive woman, but not so much in recent years. She's started getting way too thin. And in terms of acting, her other half Brad Pitt is way better and has done some more interesting roles. This movie doesn't help with her looks either. There's the heavy makeup she's wearing and she looks silly with her attempt at a sexy walk. Really I'd say Girl, Interrupted was her last real daring role where she showed off some raw emotion and showed that she can act. But most of the things she does now are just her looking pretty, or trying to be a bad@$$. Now I still need to see some of her newer films. I haven't seen Changeling yet, but my main reason for wanting to see that is because it's directed by Clint Eastwood, who is really good at working with actors and getting them to give a decent performance.<br /><br />As for Johnny Depp, I love the guy. He has a natural charm for sure. Yes in recent years he too has done more standard roles as well. But he at least still entertains me with these performances; Captain Jack Sparrow has become one of my favorite movie characters. And even in more standard films he seems to at least be putting in some effort, more than I can say for Jolie in most things. Not so much in this movie though. I'd say he was doing it for the paycheck. There's a quote by Jolie that she was just doing because she thought hey it'll be a quick shoot and she had nothing better to do. That's the secondary problem with this movie too. Neither are really putting effort in their performances. I can't really buy Depp as a simple math teacher; Depp has no business playing a normal person. His most normal performance was probably the original Nightmare on Elm Street, and that was a supernatural slasher film where he got sucked into a bed followed by a geyser of blood. The other would probably be What's Eating Gilbert Grape, but even in that he was part of dysfunctional family and surrounded by eccentrics. See both movies if you haven't, they're classics.<br /><br />The primary problem with this movie is that it's predictable. Even from the trailer it was super obvious that Depp's character was going to be this international thief, otherwise it wouldn't be exciting enough. If you're star is Depp, of course he's going to be the suspect. Course this movie fails to be exciting as everything moves slowly. I'm guessing the $100 million budget was mainly because Jolie and Depp cost a lot of money. Course I'm sure Paul Bettany and Timothy Dalton weren't cheap either. I've seen more exciting, fast paced action films that didn't cost nearly this much. The 2006 actioner Shoot 'Em Up cost $39 million to make, that's more than half of what this movie cost. Look at the first Terminator movie, it only cost $6 million. Sure it's sequel cost $100 million, BUT that movie was fast paced and awesome! The 2003 Italian Job cost $60 million and was more exciting than this; sure it wasn't a fantastic movie but it was at least fun. This movie spends most of it's time wanting to show off the Venice. Sure Venice looks great, but this is supposed to be a thriller with some action and such. It doesn't deliver very well on that. The George Clooney film The American (released the same year) cost $20 million and had some stunning Italy landscapes, and it's pace was slow too, but it did it in a way that was able to still build tension and have some mystery to it. Now I can see the American dividing opinions, but I'll still stand by what I said when I think it has a more engaging plot. Since the twist of the Tourist is obvious, it just leaves you thinking of when they're finally going to reveal when Depp finally admits he's the prime suspect. If he and Jolie had chemistry and actually put effort into their performances I could ignore that because I'd least be entertained. But they don't, and it just gets really tedious. Going back to Charade, certain things were obvious, but again it had a great pace and Hepburn and Grant had excellent chemistry and used their charms. But Depp just looks bored. Say what you will about any of the Pirates sequels, but at least then you can tell he's having a good time in those movies. Also those movies used their budgets well to give us some mind blowing special effects and awesome action scenes (the whirlpool fight in World's End is EPIC!) and the rest of the cast I'm sure see he's having fun so they try to have fun. This movie though isn't fun though, another thing that Charade has above this movie. Most of the cast seems to be trying, but it really isn't enough if your leads can't wake up and do some acting.<br /><br />That's really all I have to say. Hope I didn't ramble on too long. While this may not be super informative of the movie itself, I hope it at least entertained and I've managed to get people to watch the other movies I talked about. Seriously, any one of those movies is a lot more fun that this one.either are really putting effort in their performances. I can't really buy Depp as a simple math teacher; Depp has no business playing a normal person. His most normal performance was probably the original Nightmare on Elm Street, and that was a supernatural slasher film where he got sucked into a bed followed by a geyser of blood. The other would probably be What's Eating Gilbert Grape, but even in that he was part of dysfunctional family and surrounded by eccentrics. See both movies if you haven't, they're classics.<br /><br />The primary problem with this movie is that it's predictable. Even from the trailer it was super obvious that Depp's character was going to be this international thief, otherwise it wouldn't be exciting enough. If you're star is Depp, of course he's going to be the suspect. Course this movie fails to be exciting as everything moves slowly. I'm guessing the $100 million budget was mainly because Jolie and Depp cost a lot of money. Course I'm sure Paul Bettany and Timothy Dalton weren't cheap either. I've seen more exciting, fast paced action films that didn't cost nearly this much. The 2006 actioner Shoot 'Em Up cost $39 million to make, that's more than half of what this movie cost. Look at the first Terminator movie, it only cost $6 million. Sure it's sequel cost $100 million, BUT that movie was fast paced and awesome! The 2003 Italian Job cost $60 million and was more exciting than this; sure it wasn't a fantastic movie but it was at least fun. This movie spends most of it's time wanting to show off the Venice. Sure Venice looks great, but this is supposed to be a thriller with some action and such. It doesn't deliver very well on that. 0812984412|2.0|I don't understand why this book has gotten so many glowing five-star reviews. It was generally an enjoyable read, and some of the nested stories were quite engaging. I thought the Louisa Ray story was very well done, and I also enjoyed the Orison story. But the Adam Ewing story was slow going because of it's dialect, and I almost put the book down without making it to the second story. Sloosha's Crossin', the innermost story, is *much* worse in this regard, and I never would have waded through that laborious prose if it hadn't formed the core of a book I had already invested a lot of time in. Yes, I get that people speak differently in a dystopian future, but most writers know when to back off of dialect and give the reader a break.<br /><br />The quality of the writing ranges from good to brilliant, but it's hampered by the narrative styles employed for the various stories. The journal of Adam Ewing is not very credible as a journal, the letters from Frobisher read more like journal entries, and the Q&A interview format of the Orison story made for a rather clunky and unnatural telling. And packaging the Crossin' story as a telling of a tale to 'young uns' just forced us into unremitting dialect for what would otherwise have been a very enjoyable read. Perhaps that's in part why I enjoyed Louisa Ray so much -- Mitchell set his devices aside for this one and just told the story straight out.<br /><br />Finally, the odd structure of the book, with all the stories breaking at mid-point and then completing in the opposite order, did nothing for me. Yes, it was a clever device, but it was a cleverness that simply got in the way of the story-telling. There were  a few cross-references among the stories, and some broad hints that all the characters are really the same character re-incarnated. But in the end, the stories just stacked up and wound down. I kept waiting for some deeper reason for the odd structure. What I kept expecting was that from the center story outward, each story would provide the key to unlocking something mysterious about the inner stories it surrounded. But aside from the bit with a tattoo, there was nothing. Nada. Why go to all the work of nesting these stories if there's no underlying reason for doing so. What I thought throughout would prove to be a reward for switching from story to story just turned out to be a pointless gimmick.

I recommend this book despite some of its shortcomings after such an engrossing read.

0812982622|4.0|Generally, I think that people control their own fates, but in a country as repressive as North Korea, maybe not.  Jun Do (John Doe?) grows up in an orphanage and then finds himself buffeted from one bad situation to another.  Along the way, however, he manages to spend a year in a school where he learns English and joins a delegation of diplomatic imposters who travel to Texas.  His exposure to American culture serves him well, especially in the second half of the book.  In a nation where a single comment can cause someone to disappear, and women routinely find themselves with replacement husbands chosen by the state, Jun Do takes the place of Commander Ga, who is married to the beautiful actress Sun Moon.  North Korea's Dear Leader, Kim Jong Il, is the scriptwriter for all of Sun Moon's films, and he obviously scripts and directs the lives of all his citizens, who live in constant fear and whose knowledge of the outside world is only as accurate as the propaganda that blares from the loudspeakers in their homes.  One of the main characters in the second half is a prison interrogator who lives with his parents.  His parents must be constantly vigilant, aware that their son could turn them in for the slightest infraction; they behave like robots in his presence, never divulging any personal opinions that might be construed as seditious.  The best that the North Korean people can hope for is survival, but for what?  Physical torture, famine, loss of loved ones?  The regime recognizes that there is a strong sense of comradeship among the people that can be used as a deterrent to defection.  If someone defects, his friends and family will suffer the consequences.  Therefore, a defection has to be camouflaged as death or kidnapping or whatever.  It's hard to conceive of such a society, but the author uses vivid imagery to draw us into the horror.  One section describes some of the things the protagonist eats to keep from starving, and I found that section even harder tostomach than the physical brutality.

You can choose the one near your location so that the latency is minimal. Many other companies are either good for the east or the west, but not both, because their servers are located in only one region. U.S. and Canada are so big! The signal traveling between the east and west needs time, which is not good for VOIP. As an example, I tested in California, it takes only 34ms to ping losangles.voip.ms, while 110ms to newyork.voip.ms. So the Los Angeles server is perfect for me. However, for people living in the east coast, the New York server should be their choice.<br /><br />After one month, I noticed the static noise is sometimes intolerable even with the Panasonic phone. B0085RQLXK|3.0|It sure seems like a long time since the last Aerosmith album. The last time the \\"Toxic Twins\\" and comp. released an album of new material, George W. Bush had just been sworn in as the forty-third president of the United States, Limp Bizkit was still popular and Pierce Brosnan was still 007. After the forgettable \\"Just Push Play,\\" (2001) came the surprisingly good blues covers album \\"Honkin' on Bobo\\" (2004) and then a lot of well documented infighting, Steven Tyler's misguided venture into \\"American Idol,\\" rumors of Billy Idol replacing Tyler, two very good Joe Perry solo albums, and then, finally, a brand new album \\"Music From Another Dimension\\" (2012).<br /><br />I really wasn't sure about what to expect from this new album. In all honesty, with the exception of \\"Honkin' On Bobo,\\" I haven't really liked anything Aerosmith has released since \\"Pump\\" (1989). From the 1990s on up, the band seemed to be complacent and lazy, rehashing the same tired rockers and ballads, album after album. \\"Get a Grip\\" (1993) and \\"Nine Lives\\" (1997) had a couple of good songs, but the albums were overly slick and polished. Then the bottom came out with that godawful song from the \\"Armageddon\\" soundtrack and the very mediocre \\"Just Push Play.\\" It was as though they had transformed from an iconic, legendary hard-rock band into a geriatric Bon Jovi--music for soccer moms and children.<br /><br />After two decades of sup-par material, I was very pleased to read to read that Aerosmith had once again teamed up with Jack Douglas, who produced such Aerosmith classics as \\"Toys in the Attic\\" (1975) and \\"Rocks\\" (1976). I was equally disappointed when I read the liner notes to see that Diane Warren, Desmond Child and others co-wrote (or in Warren's case, wrote) some of the songs.<br /><br />I found \\"Music From Another Dimension\\" a hard album to rate, simply because it's very uneven. There are songs that are great; The album starts out with a trio of outstanding rockers. \\"LUV XXX,\\" which Julian Lennon provides some great harmonies, \\"Oh Yeah,\\" which is a true classic Aerosmith rocker, and the poppie-but-rocking \\"Beautiful,\\" get the album off to a great start. \\"Legendary Child\\" isn't anything groundbreaking, but it works and features a pretty sweet solo. \\"Street Jesus\\" is a pretty solid, old-school, boogie-blues rocker, \\"Freedom Fighter,\\" (feat. Joe Perry on vocals) with its' thoughtful lyrics is memorable, while \\"Something\\" (also with Joe Perry singing) is one of the album's best songs. It's a little off-beat, and it can be described as \\"Beatle-esque,\\" with its' Sgt.Pepper/Magical Mystery Tour-era background harmonies.<br /><br />So, seven of the album's fifteen tracks are very good, but then, then there are the ballads. Those dreaded Aerosmith ballads...and there are a lot of them on this album, a total of six!--\\"Tell Me,\\" \\"What Could Have Been Love,\\" \\"Can't Stop Lovin' You,\\" (with Carrie Underwood?!) \\"We All Fall Down,\\" \\"Closer\\" and \\"Another Last Goodbye.\\" They can be reviewed with just two simple words--garbage & awful. The album's other two songs \\"Lover Alot\\" and \\"Out Go the Lights\\" are decent but nothing special.<br /><br />If you are an Aerosmith fan, I would recommend \\"Music From Another Dimension\\" as it has several really good songs. Those seven songs might not be as good as anything from \\"Rocks,\\" but they are some of the best tracks that Aerosmith have released in the past thirty years. The band sound hungry, revitalized and Jack Douglas brings out the best in them. The ballads, however, are what keep \\"Music From Another Dimension\\" from being a great comeback album. The problem is that Aerosmith are so concerned with staying relevant and writing hits, they wind up with an album that is half-filled with crap. Aerosmith should take a lesson from Van Halen, who didn't care about writing a cross-over hit and released the brilliant \\"A Different Kind of Truth\\" earlier this year. I bet that Van Halen's album won't sell all that much less than Aerosmith's new one, and they didn't stoop to getting Diane Warren or Carrie Underwood to help them.ne, and they didn't stoop to getting Diane Warren or Carrie Underwood to help them.

B00571F26Y|5.0|My biggest problem with weight gain since retirement has been from overeating in the evening, especially late at night. My doctor recommended this book, as a way to reduce cravings from wheat products, which he found helpful for his own weight loss.<br /><br />This book is filled with research studies to support the ideas presented by Dr. Davis, often in great detail. He also brings up similar findings to avoid other grain products, as so much of our food supply is now genetically engineered to produce the highest yields and profits.<br /><br />Therefore, there is no chance of making our food less hazardous to our health within the next few decades. Our educational institutions, both private and government sponsored are very slow to accept research that clearly says there is something wrong with our concepts about nutrition, especially for our increasingly overweight and diabetic population. Then just add to this the untold amount of money influence from the food industry on nutrition education through advertising alone, not to mention how much is paid to the election campaigns of our law-makers to keep their industry taxation and regulation as low as possible.<br /><br />Politics aside, what I got from this book is to definitely try to keep away from wheat products, but also other carbohydrates if you want to reduce food cravings and lose weight.  I also recommend the book by Dr. Thompson called \\"The Glycemic Load Diabetes Solution\\", whether you are diabetic or heading in that direction. Watching your daily glycemic load (GL) lets you eat more of the good carbohydrates - especially those in most vegetables and fruits, and makes weight loss both easy and enjoyable. However, Dr. Thompson does not provide a formula for calculating glycemic load (GL) and provides meal plans that reflect glycemic loads that should be divided by 100, as most calculations are represented for this lower number. The usual formula is GL = GI (glycemic index)/100 x Net Carbs (carb grams, minus grams of fiber), in other words you need to know the glycemic index to calculate the load.  Dietgrail.com has a comprehensive GL list of foods, and a software download option that makes searching much easier.<br /><br />I also considered the \\"The New Atkins for a New You\\", by Westman, et al, but found that it did not consider glycemic load in its recommendations.  I felt this low-carbohydrate only approach unnecessarily restricted many vegetables and fruits, especially in the early \\"phases\\" of the plan, which may produce faster weight loss, but cravings as well. Why fight cravings, the downfall of most weight-loss programs?<br /><br />For now, I'm just calculating GLs to a total of around 50 or less each day, which is similar to limiting to 50 carbs a day, but with much greater eating satisfaction from a greater variety of whole foods. Unfortunately, there is no GL index for most commercially prepared foods, so I just estimate net carbs in these cases - and watch out for added sugars in these foods. Low-fat and low-calorie foods are usually the worst offenders with added sugars and poor value carbs. Overall, following this way of eating steers you to mostly whole food choices, which is probably a good idea. This plan produced more energy after about a week and several lost pounds. A simple plan I can easily stay with and with more food varieties and enjoyment than I have experienced in the past.  I also feel more satisfied during the day, and have much fewer cravings at night.  If they occur, a hunk of cheese or a couple hands full of nuts put them to rest.

The only summation that I'll provide is an indirect one; this book takes large parts of the plots of Komarr and A Civil Campaign, mashes them together, turns them inside out, and twists - hard.  This is not the first Barrayar story that has been centered on someone other than her main protagonist, Miles Vorkosigan, though he has been the focus of most of them.  The events of [[ASIN:1451637500 Cryoburn]] left Miles with a very much restructured life, however, and it may be difficult for Bujold to write more about him entertainingly, so it's not surprising that she has shifted to Ivan here.  He has been a background character since early on, and this is where he finally comes into his own.
